data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="text-align:right; padding-right:0.5em;">Induction</th><td style="padding-left:0.5em;">\n<a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_1956" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 1956">1956</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="text-align:right; padding-right:0.5em;">Vote</th><td style="padding-left:0.5em;">\n85% (eighth ballot)</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Henry Benjamin Greenberg</b> (born <b>Hyman Greenberg</b>; January 1, 1911 \xe2\x80\x93 September 4, 1986), nicknamed "<b>Hammerin\' Hank</b>", "<b>Hankus Pankus</b>", or "<b>The Hebrew Hammer</b>", was an American <a href="/wiki/Professional_baseball" title="Professional baseball">professional baseball</a> player and team executive. He played in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB), primarily for the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a> as a <a href="/wiki/First_baseman" title="First baseman">first baseman</a> in the 1930s and 1940s.  A member of the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">Baseball Hall of Fame</a> and a two-time <a href="/wiki/MLB_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Most Valuable Player Award">MVP</a> winner, he was one of the premier power hitters of his generation and is widely considered as one of the greatest <a href="/wiki/Slugging_percentage" title="Slugging percentage">sluggers</a> in baseball history.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> He had 47 months of military service including service in World War II, all of which took place during his major league career.<sup id="cite_ref-baseballhall_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseballhall-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Greenberg played the first twelve of his thirteen seasons in the major leagues on the Detroit team. He was an <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star</a> for four seasons<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> and an AL <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">Most Valuable Player</a> in 1935 (first baseman) and 1940 (left fielder).<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> He had a <a href="/wiki/Batting_average" title="Batting average">batting average</a> over .300 in eight seasons, and he was a member of four Tigers <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> teams which won two championships (1935 and 1945). He was the AL <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_annual_home_run_leaders" title="List of Major League Baseball annual home run leaders">home run leader</a> four times and his 58 home runs for the Tigers in 1938 equaled <a href="/wiki/Jimmie_Foxx" title="Jimmie Foxx">Jimmie Foxx</a>\'s 1932 mark for the most in one season by anyone but <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>, and tied Foxx for the most home runs between Ruth\'s record 60 in 1927 and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Maris" title="Roger Maris">Roger Maris</a>\' record 61 in 1961. Greenberg was the first major league player to hit 25 or more home runs in a season in each league, and remains the AL record-holder for most RBIs in a single season by a right-handed batter (183 in 1937, a 154-game schedule).\n</p><p>Greenberg was the first <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> superstar in American team sports.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> He attracted national attention in 1934 when he refused to play on <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur" title="Yom Kippur">Yom Kippur</a>, the holiest holiday in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, even though he was not particularly observant religiously and the Tigers were in the middle of a pennant race. In 1947, Greenberg signed a contract with a $30,000 raise to a record $85,000<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> before being sold to the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> where he played his final MLB season that year. He was one of the few opposing players to publicly welcome <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> that year to the major leagues.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li href="#Professional_baseball"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Professional baseball</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Minor_leagues"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Minor leagues</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Major_leagues"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Major leagues</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a href="#Early_years"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early years</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-6"><a href="#World_War_II_service"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">World War II service</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7"><a href="#Return_to_baseball"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Return to baseball</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-8"><a href="#Final_seasons"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Final seasons</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-9"><a href="#Management_and_ownership"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Management and ownership</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Personal_life"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Personal life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#MLB_honors"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">MLB honors</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Other_honors"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Other honors</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Miscellaneous"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Miscellaneous</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#In_media"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">In media</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Films"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Films</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Books"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Books</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Hank Greenberg was born Hyman Greenberg on January 1, 1911, in <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">Greenwich Village</a>, New York City, to <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanian Jews">Romanian-born Jewish</a> immigrant parents David and Sarah Greenberg, who owned a successful cloth-shrinking plant in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> He had two brothers, Ben, four years older, and Joe, five years younger, who also played baseball, and a sister, Lillian, two years older.<sup id="cite_ref-bookref1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookref1-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His family moved to <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">the Bronx</a> when he was about seven.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He attended <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe_High_School_(New_York)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Monroe High School (New York)">James Monroe High School</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bronx" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronx">Bronx</a>, where he was an outstanding all-around athlete and was bestowed with the long-standing nickname of "Bruggy" by his basketball coach.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosengren_p16_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosengren_p16-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His preferred sport was <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a>, and his preferred position was <a href="/wiki/First_baseman" title="First baseman">first base</a>. In high school <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>, he was on the Monroe team that won the city championship.<sup id="cite_ref-sabrbio_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sabrbio-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1929, the 18-year-old 6-foot-4-inch Greenberg was recruited by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>, who already had <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a> at first base.  Greenberg turned them down and instead attended <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> for a year, where he was a member of <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Alpha_Mu" title="Sigma Alpha Mu">Sigma Alpha Mu</a>, after which he signed with the Detroit Tigers for $9,000 ($132,000 today).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_baseball">Professional baseball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Professional baseball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_leagues">Minor leagues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Minor leagues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Greenberg played <a href="/wiki/Minor_league_baseball" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor league baseball">minor league baseball</a> for three years. Greenberg played 17 games in 1930 for the <a href="/wiki/Hartford_Senators" title="Hartford Senators">Hartford Senators</a>, then played at <a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh, North Carolina</a>, for the <a href="/wiki/Raleigh_Capitals" title="Raleigh Capitals">Raleigh Capitals</a>, where he hit .314 with 19 home runs.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1931, he played at <a href="/wiki/Evansville,_Indiana" title="Evansville, Indiana">Evansville</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Evansville_Hubs" class="mw-redirect" title="Evansville Hubs">Evansville Hubs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Illinois%E2%80%93Indiana%E2%80%93Iowa_League" title="Illinois\xe2\x80\x93Indiana\xe2\x80\x93Iowa League">Illinois\xe2\x80\x93Indiana\xe2\x80\x93Iowa League</a> (.318, 15 homers, 85 RBIs).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1932, at <a href="/wiki/Beaumont,_Texas" title="Beaumont, Texas">Beaumont</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Beaumont_Exporters" title="Beaumont Exporters">Beaumont Exporters</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Texas_League" title="Texas League">Texas League</a>, he hit 39 homers with 131 RBIs, won the MVP award, and led Beaumont to the Texas League title.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_leagues">Major leagues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Major leagues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>When he broke into the major leagues in 1930, Greenberg was the youngest MLB player (19).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1933, he rejoined the Tigers and <a href="/wiki/Batting_average" title="Batting average">hit</a> .301 while <a href="/wiki/RBI" class="mw-redirect" title="RBI">driving</a> in 87 runs.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  At the same time, he was third in the league in strikeouts (78).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg/200px-HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg" width="200" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg/300px-HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="315" data-file-height="386" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HankGreenbergGoudeycard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenberg 1934 <a href="/wiki/Goudey" title="Goudey">Goudey</a> baseball card</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1934, his second major-league season, he hit .339 and helped the Tigers reach their first <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> in 25 years.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  He led the league in doubles, with 63 (the fourth-highest all-time in a single season), and extra base hits (96).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  He was third in the AL in slugging percentage (.600) \xe2\x80\x93 behind <a href="/wiki/Jimmie_Foxx" title="Jimmie Foxx">Jimmie Foxx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a>, but ahead of <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>, and in RBIs (139), sixth in batting average (.339), seventh in home runs (26), and ninth in on-base percentage (.404).<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Late in the 1934 season, he announced that he would not play on September 10, which was <a href="/wiki/Rosh_Hashanah" title="Rosh Hashanah">Rosh Hashanah</a>, the Jewish New Year, or on September 19, the Day of Atonement, <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur" title="Yom Kippur">Yom Kippur</a>. Fans grumbled, "Rosh Hashanah comes every year but the Tigers haven\'t won the pennant since <a href="/wiki/1909_in_baseball" title="1909 in baseball">1909</a>." Greenberg did considerable soul-searching, and discussed the matter with his <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbi</a>; finally he relented and agreed to play on Rosh Hashanah, but stuck with his decision not to play on Yom Kippur. Dramatically, Greenberg hit two home runs in a 2\xe2\x80\x931 Tigers victory over Boston on Rosh Hashanah.  The next day\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Detroit_Free_Press" title="Detroit Free Press">Detroit Free Press</a></i> ran the Hebrew lettering for "Happy New Year" across its front page.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  Columnist and poet <a href="/wiki/Edgar_A._Guest" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar A. Guest">Edgar A. Guest</a> expressed the general opinion in a poem titled "Speaking of Greenberg", in which he used the Irish (and thus Catholic) names Murphy and Mulroney. The poem ends with the lines "<i>We shall miss him on the infield and shall miss him at the bat / But he\'s true to his religion\xe2\x80\x94and I honor him for that.</i>" The complete text of the poem is at the end of Greenberg\'s biography page at the website of the International Jewish Sports Hall of Fame. The Detroit press was not so kind regarding the Yom Kippur decision, nor were many fans, but Greenberg in his autobiography recalled that he received a standing ovation from congregants at the <a href="/wiki/Shaarey_Zedek" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaarey Zedek">Shaarey Zedek</a> synagogue when he arrived. Absent Greenberg, the Tigers lost to the New York Yankees, 5\xe2\x80\x932. The Tigers went on to face the St. Louis Cardinals in the <a href="/wiki/1934_World_Series" title="1934 World Series">1934 World Series</a>.\n</p><p>In 1935 Greenberg led the league in RBIs (170), total bases (389), and extra base hits (98), tied <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Foxx" class="mw-redirect" title="Jimmy Foxx">Foxx</a> for the AL title in home runs (36), was 2nd in the league in doubles (46), slugging percentage (.628), was 3rd in the league in triples (16), and in runs scored (121), 6th in on-base percentage (.411) and walks (87), and was 7th in batting average (.328).<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> He was unanimously voted the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">Most Valuable Player</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/All-Star_break_(MLB)" class="mw-redirect" title="All-Star break (MLB)">All-Star break</a> that season, Greenberg hit 25 home runs and set an MLB record (still standing) of 103 RBIs \xe2\x80\x93 but was not selected to the AL All-Star roster (both managers put themselves on the rosters but did not play).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He helped lead the Tigers to their first World Series title, but sprained his wrist in the second game and did not play in the other 4 games.\n</p><p>In 1936, Greenberg reinjured his wrist in a collision with <a href="/wiki/Jake_Powell" title="Jake Powell">Jake Powell</a> of the Washington Senators in April and did not play the remainder of the season. He finished the season with 16 hits, 1 home run, and 15 RBIs in 12 games.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg/400px-1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg" width="400" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg/600px-1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg/800px-1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7975" data-file-height="6006" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seven of the American League\'s 1937 All-Star players, from left to right <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Cronin" title="Joe Cronin">Joe Cronin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Dickey" title="Bill Dickey">Bill Dickey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Gehringer" title="Charlie Gehringer">Charlie Gehringer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmie_Foxx" title="Jimmie Foxx">Jimmie Foxx</a>, and Greenberg. All seven would be elected to the Hall of Fame.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1937, Greenberg recovered from his injury and was voted to the AL All-Star roster, but did not play. On September 19, 1937, he hit the first home run into the center field <a href="/wiki/Bleachers" class="mw-redirect" title="Bleachers">bleachers</a> at Yankee Stadium.  He led the AL by driving in 183 runs (third all-time, behind <a href="/wiki/Hack_Wilson" title="Hack Wilson">Hack Wilson</a> in 1930 and <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a> in 1931), and in extra base hits (103), while batting .337 with 200 hits.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  He was second in the league in home runs (40), doubles (49), total bases (397), slugging percentage (.668), and walks (102), third in on-base percentage (.436), and seventh in batting average (.337).<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Greenberg came in third in the vote for MVP.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:20%;&#32;; padding:10px;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">He was one of the truly great hitters, and when I first saw him at bat, he made my eyes pop out.</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a><sup id="cite_ref-bookref2_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookref2-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>A prodigious <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home run</a> hitter, Greenberg narrowly missed breaking <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>\'s single-season home run record in 1938, when he hit 58 home runs, leading the league for the second time.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> That year, he had 11 games with multiple home runs, a new major league record. <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Sosa" title="Sammy Sosa">Sammy Sosa</a> tied the record in 1998. Greenberg matched what was then the single-season home run record by a right-handed batter, (<a href="/wiki/Jimmie_Foxx" title="Jimmie Foxx">Jimmie Foxx</a>, 1932); the mark stood for 66 years until it was broken by Sammy Sosa and <a href="/wiki/Mark_McGwire" title="Mark McGwire">Mark McGwire</a>.  Greenberg also had a 59th home run washed away in a <a href="/wiki/Rainout" title="Rainout">rainout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  It has been long speculated that Greenberg was intentionally walked late in the season to prevent him from breaking Ruth\'s record, but Greenberg dismissed this speculation, calling it "crazy stories."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  Nonetheless, Howard Megdal has calculated that in September 1938, Greenberg was walked in over 20% of his plate appearances, the highest percentage in his career by far.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Megdal\'s article cited this walk percentage statistic as evidence of AL teams not wanting Greenberg to break Ruth\'s record due to anti-Semitism. However, an examination of the box scores indicate this spike in walks was due to a few games against St. Louis Browns\' pitchers with horrific control, not a general league tendency.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Greenberg was again voted to the AL All-Star roster in 1938, but because he was not named to the 1935 AL All-Star roster and was benched in the 1937 game, he declined to accept a starting position on the 1938 AL team and did not play (the NL won 4-1). He led the league in runs scored (144) and at-bats per home run (9.6), tied for the AL lead in walks (119), was second in RBIs (146), slugging percentage (.683), and total bases (380), and third in OBP (.438) and set a still-standing major league record of 39 homers in his home park, the newly reconfigured Briggs Stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He also set a major-league record with 11 multiple-home run games. He came in third in the vote for MVP.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1939 Greenberg was voted to the AL All-Star roster for the third year in a row and was a starter at first base, and singled and walked in 4 at-bats (AL won 3-1).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He finished second in the AL in home runs (33) and strikeouts (95), third in doubles (42) and slugging percentage (.622), fourth in RBIs (112), sixth in walks (91), and ninth in on-base percentage (.420).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the 1939 season ended, Greenberg was asked by general manager Jack Zeller to take a salary cut of $5,000 ($88,000 today) as a result of his off year in power and run production. He was asked to move from first base to the outfield to accommodate <a href="/wiki/Rudy_York" title="Rudy York">Rudy York</a>, who was one of the best young hitters of his generation; York was tried at catcher, third baseman, and outfielder and proved to be a defensive liability at each position. Greenberg in turn, demanded a $10,000 bonus if he mastered the outfield, insisting <i>he</i> was the one taking the risk in learning a new position. Greenberg received his bonus at the end of spring training.\n</p><p>In 1940, Greenberg switched from playing the first base position to the <a href="/wiki/Left_fielder" title="Left fielder">left field</a> position. For the 4th consecutive time, he was voted by the season\'s AL All-Star team manager to the AL All-Star team.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In the bottom of the 6th inning, Greenberg and <a href="/wiki/Lou_Finney" title="Lou Finney">Lou Finney</a> were sent into the game to replace right fielder <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Keller" title="Charlie Keller">Charlie Keller</a> and left fielder <a href="/wiki/Ted_Williams" title="Ted Williams">Ted Williams</a> with Greenberg playing in left field and Finney in right field. Greenberg batted twice in the game and fouled out to the catcher two-times. The NL won the game 4-0. That season, he led the AL in home runs for the third time in 6 years with 41; in RBIs (150), doubles (50), total bases (384), extra base hits (99), at-bats per home run (14.0), and slugging percentage (.670; 44 points ahead of <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He was second in the league behind Williams in runs scored (129) and OBP (.433), all while batting .340 (fifth best in the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">AL</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  He also led the Tigers to the AL <a href="/wiki/Pennant_(sports)" title="Pennant (sports)">pennant</a>, and won his second <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> <a href="/wiki/MVP" class="mw-redirect" title="MVP">MVP</a> award, becoming the first player in major-league history to win an MVP award at two different playing positions.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_service">World War II service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: World War II service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On October 16, 1940, Greenberg became the first American League player to register for the nation\'s first peacetime draft.<sup id="cite_ref-baseballinwartime.com_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseballinwartime.com-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 1941, the Detroit <a href="/wiki/Draft_board" title="Draft board">draft board</a> initially classified Greenberg as <a href="/wiki/4F_(military_conscription)" class="mw-redirect" title="4F (military conscription)">4F</a> for "<a href="/wiki/Flat_feet" title="Flat feet">flat feet</a>" after his first physical for military service and was recommended for light duty. The rumors that he had bribed the board, and concern that he would be likened to <a href="/wiki/Jack_Dempsey" title="Jack Dempsey">Jack Dempsey</a> who had received negative publicity for failure to serve in World War I, led Greenberg to request to be reexamined. On April 18, he was found fit for regular military service and was reclassified.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg/220px-Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg/330px-Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg/440px-Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg 2x" data-file-width="479" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenberg_being_sworn_in.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenberg taking his oath of service</div></div></div>\n<p>On May 7, 1941, he was inducted into the U.S. Army after playing left field in 19 games and reported to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Custer_Training_Center" title="Fort Custer Training Center">Fort Custer</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_Creek,_Michigan" title="Battle Creek, Michigan">Battle Creek, Michigan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-baseballinwartime.com_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseballinwartime.com-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> His salary was cut from $55,000 ($961,000 today) a year to $21 ($400 today) a month.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> He was not bitter, and stated, "I made up my mind to go when I was called. My country comes first." In November, while serving as an anti-tank gunner, he was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Sergeant" title="Sergeant">sergeant</a>, but was <a href="/wiki/Honorably_discharged" class="mw-redirect" title="Honorably discharged">honorably discharged</a> on December 5 (the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> released men aged 28 years and older from service), two days before <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a> bombed <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Greenberg re-enlisted as a sergeant on February 1, 1942, and volunteered for service in the <a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Army Air Forces">Army Air Forces</a>, becoming the first major league player to do so. He graduated from <a href="/wiki/Officer_Candidate_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Officer Candidate School">Officer Candidate School</a> and was commissioned as a <a href="/wiki/US_First_Lieutenant" class="mw-redirect" title="US First Lieutenant">first lieutenant</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Corps" title="United States Army Air Corps">Air Corps</a> (the new "Air Forces" service retaining the old name for <a href="/wiki/1941_in_aviation#June" title="1941 in aviation">its own logistics and training elements</a>) and was assigned to the Physical Education Program.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In February 1944, he was sent to the U.S. Army <a href="/wiki/Special_Services_(entertainment)" title="Special Services (entertainment)">Special Services</a> school. Promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain" title="Captain">captain</a>, he requested overseas duty later that year and served in the <a href="/wiki/China_Burma_India_Theater_of_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="China Burma India Theater of World War II">China-Burma-India Theater</a> for over six months, scouting locations for <a href="/wiki/B-29" class="mw-redirect" title="B-29">B-29</a> bomber bases and was a physical training officer with the <a href="/wiki/58th_Air_Division" title="58th Air Division">58th Bomber Wing</a>. He was a Special Services officer of the <a href="/wiki/XX_Bomber_Command" title="XX Bomber Command">20th Bomber Command</a>, <a href="/wiki/20th_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="20th Air Force">20th Air Force</a> in China when it began bombing Japan on June 15. He was ordered to New York, and in late 1944, to <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>. Greenberg served 47 months, the longest of any major league player.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_baseball">Return to baseball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Return to baseball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Greenberg remained in military uniform until he was placed on the military inactive list and discharged from the U.S. Army on June 14, 1945. He was the first major league player to return to MLB after the war. He returned to the Tigers team, and in his first game back on July 1, he homered. The <a href="/wiki/1945_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1945 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star Game</a> scheduled for July 10 had been officially cancelled on April 24 and MLB did not name All-Stars that season due to strict travel restrictions during the last days of the war with Germany and Japan and the ending of World War II. In place of the All-Star Game, seven interleague games were played (eight had been scheduled) on July 9 and 10 to benefit the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=War_Relief&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="War Relief (page does not exist)">War Relief</a> fund. An <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> All-Star roster was named (no game was played) for the AL and NL by a group of their sportswriters that included Greenberg as one of the All-Stars.<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-reference1_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-reference1-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-baseball-almanac.com_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-almanac.com-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Greenberg, who played left field in 72 games and batted .311 in 1945, helped lead the Tigers to a come-from-behind American League pennant, clinching it with a grand slam home run in the dark\xe2\x80\x94there were no lights in <a href="/wiki/Sportsman%27s_Park" title="Sportsman&#39;s Park">Sportsman\'s Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>\xe2\x80\x94ninth inning of the final game of the season. The ump\xe2\x80\x94former Yankee pitching star of the 1920s <a href="/wiki/Murderers_Row" class="mw-redirect" title="Murderers Row">Murderers Row</a> team <a href="/wiki/George_Pipgras" title="George Pipgras">George Pipgras</a>\xe2\x80\x94supposedly said, "Sorry Hank, but I\'m gonna have to call the game. I can\'t see the ball." Greenberg replied, "Don\'t worry, George, I can see it just fine", so the game continued.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Where did this quotation come from? (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It ended with Greenberg\'s grand slam on the next pitch, clinching <a href="/wiki/Hal_Newhouser" title="Hal Newhouser">Hal Newhouser</a>\'s 25th victory of the season.  His home run allowed the Tigers to clinch the pennant and avoid a one-game playoff (that would have been necessary without the win) against the now-second-place Washington Senators. The Tigers went on to beat the Cubs in the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> in seven games. Only three home runs were hit in that World Series. <a href="/wiki/Phil_Cavarretta" title="Phil Cavarretta">Phil Cavarretta</a> hit a home run for the Cubs in Game One, Greenberg hit a homer in Game Two, where he batted in three runs in a 4\xe2\x80\x931 Tigers win, and he hit a two-run homer in Game Six in the eighth inning that tied the score 8\xe2\x80\x938; the Cubs went on to win that game with a run in the bottom of the 12th.\n</p><p>In 1946, he returned to peak form and playing at first base. He led the AL in home runs (44) and RBIs (127), both for the fourth time.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He was second in slugging percentage (.604) and total bases (316) behind <a href="/wiki/Ted_Williams" title="Ted Williams">Ted Williams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1947, Greenberg and the Tigers had a lengthy salary dispute. When Greenberg decided to retire rather than play for less, Detroit sold his contract to the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a>. To persuade him not to retire, Pittsburgh made Greenberg the first baseball player to earn over $80,000 ($877,000 today) in a season as pure salary (though the exact amount is a matter of some dispute). Team co-owner <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a> recorded a song, "Goodbye, Mr. Ball, Goodbye" with <a href="/wiki/Groucho_Marx" title="Groucho Marx">Groucho Marx</a> and Greenberg to celebrate Greenberg\'s arrival. The Pirates also reduced the size of <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Field" title="Forbes Field">Forbes Field</a>\'s cavernous left field, renaming the section "Greenberg Gardens" to accommodate Greenberg\'s pull-hitting style. Greenberg played first base for the Pirates in 1947 and was one of the few opposing players to publicly welcome <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> to the majors.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hank_Greenberg_Pirates.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Hank_Greenberg_Pirates.png/190px-Hank_Greenberg_Pirates.png" width="190" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Hank_Greenberg_Pirates.png 1.5x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hank_Greenberg_Pirates.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenberg with the Pirates in 1947</div></div></div>\n<p>That year he also had a chance to mentor a young future Hall-of-Famer, the 24-year-old <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Kiner" title="Ralph Kiner">Ralph Kiner</a>. Said Greenberg, "Ralph had a natural home run swing. All he needed was somebody to teach him the value of hard work and self-discipline. Early in the morning on off-days, every chance we got, we worked on hitting."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  Kiner would go on to hit 51 home runs that year to lead the National League.\n</p><p>In his final season of 1947, Greenberg tied for the league lead in walks with 104, with a .408 on-base percentage and finished eighth in the league in home runs and tenth in slugging percentage.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Greenberg became the first major league player to hit 25 or more home runs in a season in each league. <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mize" title="Johnny Mize">Johnny Mize</a> became the second in 1950.\n</p><p>Nevertheless, Greenberg retired as a player to take a front-office post with the Cleveland Indians.  No player had ever retired after a final season in which they hit so many home runs.  Since then, only Ted Williams (1960, 29), <a href="/wiki/Dave_Kingman" title="Dave Kingman">Dave Kingman</a> (1986; 35), <a href="/wiki/Mark_McGwire" title="Mark McGwire">Mark McGwire</a> (2001; 29), <a href="/wiki/Barry_Bonds" title="Barry Bonds">Barry Bonds</a> (2007; 28) and <a href="/wiki/David_Ortiz" title="David Ortiz">David Ortiz</a> (2016; 38) have hit as many or more homers in their final season.\n</p><p>Through 2010, he was first in career home runs and RBIs (ahead of <a href="/wiki/Shawn_Green" title="Shawn Green">Shawn Green</a>) and batting average (ahead of <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Braun" title="Ryan Braun">Ryan Braun</a>), and fourth in hits (behind <a href="/wiki/Lou_Boudreau" title="Lou Boudreau">Lou Boudreau</a>), among all-time Jewish major league baseball players.<sup id="cite_ref-JML_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JML-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a fielder, the 193-cm (6-foot-4-inch) Greenberg was awkward and unsure of himself early in his career, but mastered first base through countless hours of practice.  Over the course of his career he demonstrated a higher-than-average fielding percentage and range at first base.  When asked to move to <a href="/wiki/Left_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Left field">left field</a> in 1940 to make room for <a href="/wiki/Rudy_York" title="Rudy York">Rudy York</a>, he worked tirelessly to conquer that position as well, reducing his errors in the outfield from 15 in 1940 to 0 in 1945.\n</p><p>Greenberg felt that runs batted in were more important than home runs.  He would tell his teammates, "just get on base", or "just get the runner to third", and he would do the rest.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_seasons">Final seasons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Final seasons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Greenberg would likely have approached 500 home runs and 1,800 RBIs had he not served in the military.<sup id="cite_ref-bullock2004_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bullock2004-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> As it was, he compiled 331 home runs and 1,276 RBI in a 1,394-game career.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  Greenberg also hit for average, earning a lifetime batting average of .313.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Starring as a first baseman and <a href="/wiki/Outfielder" title="Outfielder">outfielder</a> with the Tigers (1930, 1933\xe2\x80\x9346) and doing duty only briefly with the Pirates (1947), Greenberg played only nine full seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  He missed all but 19 games of the 1941 season, the three full seasons that followed, and most of 1945 to World War II military service and missed most of another season with a broken wrist.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Management_and_ownership">Management and ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Management and ownership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After the 1947 season, Greenberg retired from the field to become the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Indians" title="Cleveland Indians">Cleveland Indians</a>\' farm system director and two years later, their <a href="/wiki/General_manager_(baseball)" title="General manager (baseball)">General Manager</a> and part-owner along with <a href="/wiki/Bill_Veeck" title="Bill Veeck">Bill Veeck</a>.  During his tenure, he sponsored more <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> players than any other major league executive.  Greenberg\'s contributions to the Cleveland farm system led to the team\'s successes throughout the 1950s, although <a href="/wiki/Bill_James" title="Bill James">Bill James</a> once wrote that the Indians\' late 1950s collapse should also be attributed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 1949, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Doby" title="Larry Doby">Larry Doby</a> also recommended Greenberg scout three players Doby used to play with in the <a href="/wiki/Negro_leagues" class="mw-redirect" title="Negro leagues">Negro leagues</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Banks" title="Ernie Banks">Ernie Banks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Mays" title="Willie Mays">Willie Mays</a>. The next offseason Doby asked what Indians\' scouts said about his recommendations. Said Greenberg, "Our guys checked \'em out and their reports were not good. They said that Aaron has a hitch in his swing and will never hit good pitching. Banks is too slow and didn\'t have enough range [at shortstop], and Mays can\'t hit a curveball."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> When Veeck sold his interest, Greenberg remained as general manager and part-owner until 1957.  He was the mastermind behind a potential move of the club to <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a> that was vetoed by the rest of ownership at the last minute. Greenberg was furious and sold his share soon afterwards.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 1959, Greenberg and Veeck teamed up for a second time when their syndicate purchased the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a>; Veeck served as team president with Greenberg as vice president and general manager. During Veeck and Greenberg\'s first season, the White Sox won their first AL pennant since 1919. Veeck would sell his shares in the White Sox in 1961, and Greenberg stepped down as general manager on August 26 of that season.<sup id="cite_ref-sabrbio_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sabrbio-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/1960_in_baseball" title="1960 in baseball">1960 season</a>, the American League announced plans to put a team in Los Angeles.  Greenberg immediately became the favorite to become the new team\'s first owner and persuaded Veeck to join him as his partner.  However, when <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Dodgers</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Walter_O%27Malley" title="Walter O&#39;Malley">Walter O\'Malley</a> got wind of these developments, he threatened to scuttle the whole deal by invoking his exclusive rights to operate a major league team in southern California.  In truth, O\'Malley wanted no part of competing against an expansion team owned by a master promoter such as Veeck, even if he was only a minority partner.  Greenberg wouldn\'t budge and pulled out of the running for what became the Los Angeles Angels (now the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Angels_of_Anaheim" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Angeles Angels of Anaheim">Los Angeles Angels of Anaheim</a>).  Greenberg later became a successful <a href="/wiki/Investment_banker" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment banker">investment banker</a>, briefly returning to baseball as a minority partner with Veeck when the latter repurchased the White Sox in 1975.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Greenberg married Caral Gimbel (daughter of <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Gimbel" title="Bernard Gimbel">Bernard Gimbel</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gimbel%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Gimbel&#39;s">Gimbel\'s</a> New York department store family) on February 18, 1946, three days after signing a $60,000 ($753,000 today) contract with the Tigers. The couple had three children\xe2\x80\x94sons <a href="/wiki/Glenn_H._Greenberg" title="Glenn H. Greenberg">Glenn H. Greenberg</a> and Stephen and a daughter, Alva\xe2\x80\x94before divorcing in 1958. Their son, Stephen, played five years in the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Senators_(1961%E2%80%9371)" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Senators (1961\xe2\x80\x9371)">Washington Senators</a>/<a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> organization.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In 1995, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stephen_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stephen Greenberg (page does not exist)">Stephen Greenberg</a> co-founded <a href="/wiki/Classic_Sports_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="Classic Sports Network">Classic Sports Network</a> with <a href="/wiki/Brian_Bedol" title="Brian Bedol">Brian Bedol</a>, which was purchased by <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> and became <a href="/wiki/ESPN_Classic" title="ESPN Classic">ESPN Classic</a>. He also was the chairman of <a href="/wiki/CBS_College_Sports_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="CBS College Sports Network">CSTV</a>, the first cable network devoted exclusively to college sports.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hank\'s grandson Spencer Greenberg is a <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> scientist and <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> entrepreneur.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In 1966, Greenberg married Mary Jo Tarola, a minor actress who appeared on-screen as Linda Douglas, and remained with her until his death. They had no children.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="MLB_honors">MLB honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: MLB honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table style="width: 100px; font-size: 90%; float:right; border: 2px solid #aaa; margin: 0em 0em 0em 1em;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenberg_DET.png" class="image"><img alt="Greenberg DET.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Greenberg_DET.png/95px-Greenberg_DET.png" width="95" height="63" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Greenberg_DET.png/143px-Greenberg_DET.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Greenberg_DET.png/190px-Greenberg_DET.png 2x" data-file-width="209" data-file-height="138" /></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">Hank Greenberg\'s number 5 was <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_retired_numbers" title="List of Major League Baseball retired numbers">retired</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers#Retired_numbers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a> in 1983.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/All-Star" class="mw-redirect" title="All-Star">All-Star</a> (AL), 1937\xe2\x80\x931940.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/MLB_Most_Valuable_Player_award" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Most Valuable Player award">Most Valuable Player (AL)</a>, 1935 and 1940.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">National Baseball Hall of Fame</a>, 1956 (First player of Jewish descent elected to the Hall of Fame garnering 85% of the votes)</li>\n<li>Sunday June 12, 1983: The Detroit Tigers retired Greenberg\'s uniform number 5 during "Greenberg-Gehringer Day" at <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Stadium_(Detroit)" title="Tiger Stadium (Detroit)">Tiger Stadium</a> (former teammate <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Gehringer" title="Charlie Gehringer">Charlie Gehringer</a>\'s uniform number 2 was also retired). Both Greenberg and Gehringer attended the ceremony.</li>\n<li>1999: <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> All-Century Team nominee (he ranked Number 37 on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sporting_News" class="mw-redirect" title="The Sporting News">The Sporting News</a>\'</i> list of the 100 Greatest Baseball Players).</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_honors">Other honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Associated Press All-Star (AL), 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-baseball-almanac.com_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-almanac.com-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/International_Jewish_Sports_Hall_of_Fame" title="International Jewish Sports Hall of Fame">International Jewish Sports Hall of Fame</a>, 1979.</li>\n<li>Jewish American Hall of Fame, 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Sports_Hall_of_Fame" title="Michigan Sports Hall of Fame">Michigan Sports Hall of Fame</a>, 1958.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/National_Jewish_Sports_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="National Jewish Sports Hall of Fame">National Jewish Sports Hall of Fame</a>, 1996.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous">Miscellaneous</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Miscellaneous">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Incidents of anti-Semitism Greenberg faced included having players stare at him and having racial slurs thrown at him by spectators and sometimes opposing players.  Examples of these imprecations were: "Hey Mo!" (referring to the Jewish prophet <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>) and "Throw a pork chop\xe2\x80\x94he can\'t hit that!"<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> (a reference to Judaic <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">kosher</a> laws).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1935_World_Series" title="1935 World Series">1935 World Series</a> umpire <a href="/wiki/George_Moriarty" title="George Moriarty">George Moriarty</a> warned some <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a> players to stop yelling anti-Semitic slurs at Greenberg and eventually cleared the players from the Cubs bench. Moriarty was disciplined for this action by then-commissioner <a href="/wiki/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis" title="Kenesaw Mountain Landis">Kenesaw Mountain Landis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fleischman_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleischman-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:42em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">When I was playing, I used to resent being singled out as a Jewish ballplayer.  I wanted to be known as a great ballplayer, period.  I\'m not sure why or when I changed, because I\'m still not a particularly religious person.  Lately, though, I find myself wanting to be remembered not only as a great ballplayer, but even more as a great Jewish ballplayer.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94 Hank Greenberg, after his career</cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Greenberg befriended Jackie Robinson after he signed with the Dodgers in 1947, and encouraged him; Robinson credited Greenberg with helping him through the difficulties of his rookie year.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Greenberg died of <a href="/wiki/Metastatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastatic">metastatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Kidney_cancer" title="Kidney cancer">kidney cancer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_California" title="Beverly Hills, California">Beverly Hills, California</a>, in 1986, and his remains were entombed at <a href="/wiki/Hillside_Memorial_Park_Cemetery" title="Hillside Memorial Park Cemetery">Hillside Memorial Park Cemetery</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California" title="Culver City, California">Culver City, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_14-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an article in 1976 in <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">Esquire</a></i> magazine, sportswriter <a href="/wiki/Harry_Stein_(author)" title="Harry Stein (author)">Harry Stein</a> published an "All Time All-Star Argument Starter", consisting of five ethnic baseball teams. Greenberg was the first baseman on Stein\'s Jewish team.\n</p><p>In 2006, Greenberg was featured on a United States <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The stamp is one of a block of four honoring "baseball sluggers", the others being <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mel_Ott" title="Mel Ott">Mel Ott</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Campanella" title="Roy Campanella">Roy Campanella</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_media">In media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Films">Films</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span Greenberg</a></i> directed by Aviva Kempner, Copyright 2013 The Ciesla Foundation, which won a <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-46" experiences from youth to adulthood.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hank_Greenberg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist"
